9889979942|5.0|Tom Carter's book `China - A Portrait of a People' is a breath of fresh air. As a post-doctoral researcher of Chinese affairs at the University of Tokyo, it is unfortunate to see so many books neglect the visual realities of life inside the world's most populous state. All too often, scholars and observers are bombarded with cold facts and descriptions which, though impressive in their detail, do not adequately portray the color, richness, diversity, and complexities of everyday life for the PRC's citizens. Carter's book does justice to the expression `A picture tells a thousand stories' with its carefully balanced and comprehensive montage of the daily joys, tragedies, routines, and hardships of the rising dragon's 1.3 billion people, 56 ethnic groups, and 33 distinct regions. Each colorful page is accompanied with a brief story about that page's characters and location as well as the author's hands-on accounts of his nationwide experiences over the course of four years.  Of particular interest was Carter's stark and sometimes shocking visual portrayal of the evident challenges and contrasts within the rising dragon as China's leaders attempt with increasing difficulty to maintain one-party rule, to address the striking wealth divide, and to hold the state together amid unprecedented changes.  The plight of a severely burned and uninsured factory worker (Page 26) sharply collides with the life of a tattooed and care-free urban teen depicted on Page 523. For all these reasons, this book goes beyond the word heavy and often generalized guides written from behind the safety of a western desk. As a teacher and a researcher, I would gladly recommend this book to those seeking a sincere understanding of those who make China what it is, its people.

1400063515|1.0|Taleb presents a great idea. Outliers are important. Often they have changed human history.<br /><br />But they are important because they are outliers. In probability theory common things happen commonly. That also means that sometimes uncommon things also happen. Sometimes these are big events, that is also understandable and completely fits the concept. Since some uncommon events are big and because they are unforeseen they can have huge consequences. Just remember a whole lot of small but uncommon events also happened and we simply ignored them.<br /><br />So while Taleb's assertion that some uncommon and big events have huge impact on human history is interesting and true, this is hardly the basis to abandon the understanding that common things happen commonly. That is what he keeps arguing throughout the book and is tedius.<br /><br />The style of writing is simply awful. For exactly 200 pages the authors keeps on giving examples and making the point that uncommon events are so big that we should probably ignore the common ones. This is liberally interspersed with dumping on just about anyone who is not Nassim Taleb. This was irritating since most of the time the author really doesnt argue ideas but simply just dumps.<br /><br />That said. I want my $10 and 3 hours back. 0375869026|5.0|Okay, I'll admit it. I don't normally read middle grade fiction, even as I begin the process of co-writing a middle grades book with a darling friend of mine (and another book blogger). Maybe it was her insistence that MG had stuff to discover that drew me to try a MG book, or the suggestion of this book from a friend on a website I commonly visit. All I knew going into this was that it made some people cry and that it was about a little boy with a facial deformity. Not my usual reading fare at all, but this book really surprised me.<br /><br />WONDER is about August Pullman, a boy beginning fifth grade and school for the first time. The reason? He was born with a genetic defect that left his face rather unsightly (according to most of the people he has encountered in his life). I did some research and, while never explicitly mentioned, I wonder if he has Treacher-Collins Syndrome or something similar. Google it if you're curious! Anyway, August is starting his first year of school at Beecher Academy, where the kids have never been exposed to someone like him. The story is about his trials and tribulations, along with the experiences of the young people around him, as he makes his way through this brave new world and the life that awaits him.<br /><br />Auggie has never known any different than what he is. He was born with a strange face and grew up with it. People have always treated him like he was a monster, from little children to adults. But he is a strong character, powerful in mind and conviction. Being sent to school is something he is hesitant about, especially for the first few months, but he grows and changes - just as the people around him grow and change.<br /><br />The story is told in a number of points of view - August, his sister Via, schoolmates Jack and Summer, Via's boyfriend Justin, and Miranda, Via's old best friend. From these people, the story of August is painted, from the friendships he has to the experiences of what it takes to know and love him. As the reader, we're exposed to every angle of the story, even though at least one POV wasn't really necessary (Justin). The differentiation between narration also ignored me. All six sounded very similar in tone, although Justin's POV was...weird.<br /><br />What I had a problem with in this story was the fact that the story was neatly tied up in a perfect bow. I won't give things away, but the story became predictable in its message from page one. It's a MG book and of course MG isn't going to go down the dark road taken by adult stories. Oh, wait, does this spoil the story? Well, let me just say it's predictable. End of story.<br /><br />This story did not shy away from the hard-hitting topics otherwise. Readers play witness to bullying, intimidation, fear, childhood drama, and medical conditions that drag the reader into the hard life our protagonist and his family has had. This story is touching, heartbreaking, and uplifting all in one.<br /><br />Oh, and I should mention that I actually cried. But it wasn't because of Auggie himself. I won't spoil the story, but it really struck a chord with me (the event) because the exact same thing happened to me.<br /><br />Even if you are not a fan of middle grade fiction, WONDER is worth a look. I believe most readers will enjoy this story through and through - the characters, the plot, the setting of a fifth grade full of turmoil. This was a great introduction for me into the world of MG fiction, and although I don't think I will become a HUGE reader in the field, I do plan on trying to read more into the lives of tweens entering the world of middle school, which I admit, I personally enjoyed a lot more than high school.<br /><br />VERDICT: Touching and warming, WONDER is a unique take on something many fear to tread on. A must read for newcomers to today's middle grade fiction.ve him. As the reader, we're exposed to every angle of the story, even though at least one POV wasn't really necessary (Justin). 0439023521|4.0|In a distant future, after war has destroyed the world as we know it today, what was formerly known as North America is now Panem. Panem is made up of 12 districts ruled under a totalitarian authority. Katniss Everdeen is our heroine, who lives with her mother and younger sister in the rustic District 12. In this future world, the authority holds what are known as the Hunger Games each year, where two youths from each district are picked at random to compete to the death. Sounds brutal, but this is how the government reminds the population that they still have control.<br /><br />Author Suzanne Collins has created a delightful futuristic world with Panem. We have several diverse districts; some having little to no technology, while others are overwhelmed with sophistication and wacky clothes to match. When Katniss ends up being a player in the games, her journey in preparation for the games is one full of colorful characters, bizarre foods, and lots of unique sight seeing. While some areas described give us the notion that the future world is a desolate and miserable wasteland, those Katniss visits throughout the games show us that we've also made quite a few interesting advancements to say the least.<br /><br />The book really kicks into high gear when the games begin. There is not a dull or boring moment once things get going. At the beginning of the book I found myself not liking Katniss; I felt she was, well, a bit of a bitch for lack of a better term. But as the games progress, they cause a bit of self-discovery for her and as the reader we really begin to cheer for her. The other characters we meet are also likable and the enemies in the games are as gruesome as you can imagine.<br /><br />Collins' writing style is just the way I like it: short and to the point while also adding that level of sophistication that tells you you're not reading a kid's book, but also doesn't show off like a thesaurus was constantly on stand-by. The detail given allows us to visualize first hand the manufactured and controlled battlegrounds during the games and gives us perfect images of devices and objects that have not yet come to existence.<br /><br />The book is geared towards younger readers, but older readers should not let this deter them from picking it up. In fact, it's tough to tell what age is actually appropriate for this book. With the entire concept of the book being based around violence, it's definitely a young adult read, but the themes are socially relevant and would appeal to many adults as well. If you're looking for something with high action, political turmoil, a touch of sci-fi, and even a slight romance angle, The Hunger Games will not disappoint.<br /><br />The book is the first of a trilogy which is also currently being developed as a film series at the time of this review. It will certainly be interesting to see how the films play out, as with the many non-existent items and all. The second and third books have since been released, Catching Fire and Mockingjay, which have been getting good reviews as well. With the gaining popularity and upcoming films, it will not surprise me that more will be heard about the series, and Collins will be much deserving of her accolades for crafting such an exciting modern adventure.<br /><br />Source: criticnic.com 25-November-2010

Marks meets Katie (Kirsten Dunst) and they fall in love. They move to Vermont to run a health food store called All Good Things, however they soon return to New York after David's father's urges for him to be a part of the family business. It's at this point things start getting interesting. Once David and Katie get married, things really start getting strange.<br /><br />David soon shows signs of some kind of mental disturbance. From talking to himself, to distancing himself from Katie, it's clear that something isn't right. Gosling handles the many different sides of David with eerie accuracy. The bouts between David and Katie are emotional and intense, which really start to set the tone for the rest of the film.<br /><br />The chemistry with Dunst and Gosling is great (well, despite the dark circumstances that take place). I can't say Kirsten Dunst has done a lot to get my attention since The Virgin Suicides, but in this film she did an excellent job and really had me feeling for Katie. We see glimpses of shady real estate escapades in the background as well as a status-loving family and the pressure that can have. The second half of the film also turns into a dark crime mystery with multiple murders and curious hints placed throughout different scenes. All of these events comprise a bizarre character study that is even more fascinating when considering the facts of which it is based.<br /><br />I found it surprising I haven't heard more about this film, especially considering how \\"in\\" Gosling is these days. B006OB3K56|4.0|Back when I watched Hunger, I recall being extremely moved by both the subject matter and the tragically beautiful imagery from director Steve McQueen. For his second feature, McQueen again tackles a controversial subject and relies on the use images and feelings to capture it.<br /><br />McQueen wisely uses his star from Hunger in Shame. Michael Fassbender plays Brandon, a successful and attractive New Yorker struggling with sex addiction. What I loved about Fassbender in Hunger was that he really was able to evoke so much feeling without having to speak, something not many actors can do well. In Shame he is able to achieve the same effect, with each look conveying a multitude of emotions.<br /><br />The character of Brandon lives a fairly routine life. Apart from his seemingly successful career and a normal social life involving having drinks at upscale cocktail bars, we're shown a more up-close look at his life of a sex addict, a darker side that he shields off from the outside world. He masturbates frequently, constantly looks at pornography, and has sex with girls from the bar to call-girls. While parts of that may sound like the life of an oversexed college student, here it's anything but funny or glorified.<br /><br />Brandon's routine is disrupted when he's paid an unexpected (and unwanted) visit from his sister Sissy (Carey Mulligan). From the moment Brandon plays and ignores messages on his answering machine from Sissy to her arrival in his apartment, we wonder why he's so heartless towards his sister. As the film progresses, there are hints that they have experienced painful childhoods, though nothing more is ever revealed.<br /><br />The dysfunctional relationship of Sissy and Brandon is a puzzling one, which leaves us wanting to know more. Every interaction they have, whether it be a look across a room or an uncomfortable heated exchange with a naked Brandon on top of Sissy, we can tell there's a lot to know. The two actors do a great job of keeping thedrama believable and McQueen does an equally great job of not spelling things out for us while keeping things interesting.<br /><br />Brandon also meets a woman named Marianne (Nicole Beharie) who causes an additional a stir in Brandon's life. She seems different than his usual encounters. Is he capable of a real relationship? This is one of many questions I asked myself. Between Sissy and Marianne, Brandon finds himself facing some personal dilemmas that we gather he's not at all used to. The ensuing drama had me feeling all sorts of emotions, but by the end I did begin to feel a little bit of hope.<br /><br />Most people will be talking about this film for its graphic sex scenes, but the film really is first and foremost a psychological drama. Brandon is a very enigmatic and fascinating character whose every action has our attention. Initially, he kind of seems like a mere pervert, but as the film progresses I really started to realize and try to understand his disease. Sex addiction is often joked about, but if there are people like Brandon out there, I think it's something that deserves to be taken seriously.<br /><br />In addition to being psychologically stimulating material, Shame is also superbly crafted in such a way that each scene captures the essence of the characters and their moods. Much like Hunger, Shame doesn't just look pretty, but the gorgeous shots also say something about each scene. From a simple back-and-forth scene with Brandon and Sissy while Sissy sings in a restaurant, to a tracking shot with Brandon running, I felt closer to the characters without them having to even do much of anything.<br /><br />Shame does for erotica what Drive did for car movies. While Shame has some otherwise steamy sex scenes, in context they're rather not sexy at all. The film is about more than the sex, Shame is a character study about a man and his internal struggles. To simply write this film off as an erotic escapade would be unfortunate. While the film is often difficult to watch and leaves us with more questions than answers, it's a fascinating drama with a lot to appreciate.<br />Source: criticnic.com 21-April-2012<br />Disclosure: Film sent by 20th Century Fox

0307346617|4.0|Like many other readers, I was first introduced to Max Brooks zombie world when I read THE ZOMBIE SURVIVAL GUIDE: RECORDED ATTACKS which first appeared as a section of the ZOMBIE SURVIVAL GUIDE and was then later turned into a graphic novel on its own.  I was impressed by the idea and faux history behind the stories.  Therefore, I was looking forward to reading WORLD WAR Z.<br /><br />WORLD WAR Z is a fictional oral history. It consists of a series of \\"interviews\\" with survivors of World War Z; the world's war against zombies. Through a wide variety of interviews from all around the world, the book examines how the outbreak began (no one knows for sure, but it started in Asia, particularly in China), how countries first dealt with the outbreak, what was done to contain the outbreak, the first terrible defeats of humanity, how people survived during the early years of the world, and eventually how humanity fought back and stopped the zombie threat.<br /><br />Brooks attempts to tie the events of the fictional zombie war with actual historical events and news headlines. For instance, there's reference to Iran obtaining a nuclear bomb and what they ultimately ended up doing with it. However, at the same time, the author politicizes some of those events. For instance, it's suggested that morale with U.S. troops was rather low because of an unpopular war in the Persian Gulf. WORLD WAR Z was published in 2006, so with that time frame in mind and other clues dropped throughout the book, it seems clear that the author is referring to the U.S. led war in Iraq.<br /><br />The concept of an oral history for a fictional event is an interesting one. It's been done before and will be done again. However, Brooks seems to have written the fictional oral history of the era.<br /><br />I enjoyed reading the book. I like reading oral histories of real-world events and I've toyed with the idea of an oral history for a couple of books I've worked on in the past.<br /><br />With that said, an oral history is not the type of book that appeals to a large audience, even if it is about a worldwide war against zombies. Oral histories are told from several different people who have different perspectives of an event. Therefore, there is no overarching narrative; there is no overall story. In real life, oral histories are wonderful devices because they provide one with a greater historical perspective. In a work of fiction, the idea doesn't translate as well. For one, many people read fiction as a form of escapism; they just want to read a good story. There is no one story in WORLD WAR Z.<br /><br />Another problem WORLD WAR Z is that there are no central characters. There's humanity and then there's zombies. However, there are no real heroes and no real villains. This is because the oral history device doesn't allow for any characterization. The best zombie stories, as Robert Kirkman has shown in \\"The Walking Dead\\" revolve around characterization and the relationships between the characters. WORLD WAR Z doesn't feature any of that. By the time the reader gets familiar with one person, the interview ends and the author has moved on to someone else, in a different part of the world.<br /><br />The oral history device also becomes an issue for those who are unfamiliar with it or who don't like it. Personally, I enjoyed WORLD WAR Z. However, there are people I know who have read the book and hated it because they don't like works of non-fiction. I also know people who have read it and said it was just ok because they didn't like the way the book was written.<br /><br />I enjoyed WORLD WAR Z. I'll admit that I was a little disappointed when I started reading it to discover that it wasn't a typical piece of fiction. However, I was already familiar with oral histories, so it didn't take me long to appreciate what the author had done. It's not the greatest piece of zombie fiction I've ever read or seen, but it's not bad.d, an oral history is not the type of book that appeals to a large audience, even if it is about a worldwide war against zombies. Oral histories are told from several different people who have different perspectives of an event. Therefore, there is no overarching narrative; there is no overall story. In real life, oral histories are wonderful devices because they provide one with a greater historical perspective. In a work of fiction, the idea doesn't translate as well. For one, many people read fiction as a form of escapism; they just want to read a good story. There is no one story in WORLD WAR Z.<br /><br />Another problem WORLD WAR Z is that there are no central characters. There's humanity and then there's zombies. However, there are no real heroes and no real villains. This is because the oral history device doesn't allow for any characterization. The best zombie stories, as Robert Kirkman has shown in \\"The Walking Dead\\" revolve around characterization and the relationships between the characters. WORLD WAR Z doesn't feature any of that. By the time the reader gets familiar with one person, the interview ends and the author has moved on to someone else, in a different part of the world.<br /><br />The oral history device also becomes an issue for those who are unfamiliar with it or who don't like it. Personally, I enjoyed WORLD WAR Z. However, there are people I know who have read the book and hated it because they don't like works of non-fiction. I also know people who have read it and said it was just ok because they didn't like the way the book was written.<br /><br />I enjoyed WORLD WAR Z. I'll admit that I was a little disappointed when I started reading it to discover that it wasn't a typical piece of fiction. However, I was already familiar with oral histories, so it didn't take me long to appreciate what the author had done. It's not the greatest piece of zombie fiction I've ever read or seen, but it's not bad.

0812992792|1.0|\\"The Orphan Master's Son Has No Clothes\\" -- I'd love to take credit for coming up with that beautifully stated, extremely accurate summing up of this awful, awful book, but I can't. For one of the first times, dinosaurs look real. Mixing animatronics and CGI every carnivore is menacing as hell. He and his scientists have found a way to clone extinct plants and animal DNA using blood from mosquitoes captured in amber millions of years ago. He brings dino-diggers Alan Grant (Sam Neill) and Ellie Sattler (Laura Dern) and mathematician Ian Malcolm (Jeff Goldblum) to the island to help him convince his investors represented by attorney Donald Gennaro (Martin Ferrero) that the idea is solid and that is successfully nearing completion.<br /><br />Several monkey-wrenches are thrown into the mix to put everyone at risk from the meat eaters. To complicate matters further Hammond's 2 spunky grandchildren are along for the ride adding a different element of risk.<br /><br />This review is from: THE LOST WORLD: JURASSIC PARK (3.0 Stars)<br /><br />If this film was original, perhaps it would have been perceived to be better than it is. The Stan Winston dinosaurs are more plentiful and even more convincing.<br /><br />In this story we find out that while the original Jurassic Park and its animals were destroyed, John Hammond (Richard Attenborough) had another nearby island where the actual cloning took place before the animals were removed to the park. His company is now in bankruptcy and is run by his no-good nephew (Arliss Howard) who wants to capture and move the animals to San Diego. So Hammond recruits Ian Malcolm (Jeff Goldblum) again to lead a crew who would photograph and document the animals before the evil nephew gets there. Malcolm (a survivor from the first film) refuses to go until he is told his girlfriend Sarah (Julianne Moore) is already on the island. So off he goes with 2 other crew members, Nick (Vince Vaughn) and Eddie (Richard Schiff).<br /><br />While the original kids, Lex and Tim, show up for a cameo appearance early in the film, they don't make the trip. So the kid factor comes in the way of Malcolm's stowaway teenage daughter Kelly (Vanessa Lee Chester). The fact that she's black and Malcolm isn't doesn't come up until Vaughn's character says to another, \\"does anyone see a family resemblance?\\" So I guess that's addressed.<br /><br />There aresome exciting scenes in this sequel. One involves Sarah and Nick deciding to treat a baby T-Rex who has a broken leg. They take it back to the trailer at the base camp. Gee, neither of these scientists thought that perhaps the infants cries might attract mom and/or dad. After ignoring the phone in the trailer that would have warned them of their obvious peril, dino-dad and mom let the scientists know what they thought of the idea of doctoring their kid.<br /><br />Except for a few humorous quips from Goldblum's character, the film is hampered by ridiculous dialog and even more ridiculous circumstances. Some of these are the equivalent of the teenagers in a horror movie who don't leave the haunted house when they have a chance. B001MW0J2O|2.0|My attention was drawn to Animal Collective by the use of their track \\"Summertime Clothes\\" in [[ASIN:B002ASVQYO a mix]] by DJ James Lavelle, where it worked quite well. I was looking forward to it, having heard that Animal Collective is exploring a psychedelic style that looks back to the 1960s, an era I'm keenly interested in.<br /><br />Electronic effects are wildly piled on top of each other. An overall tonality is maintained, but every instrumental part seems to be going its own direction. But unfortunately, I'm quite disappointed by the album. The band's psychedelic stylings seem a bit recherch&eacute; instead of the actual result of psychedelic experiences. Perhaps the biggest flaw are the vocals, which are fairly pop-sounding and aren't zany like the music would demand. To my ears, it sounds like a boy band is singing over musical textures made by someone else. I also feel that there's a lack of variety on the album; so many tracks are cut from the very same cloth.<br /><br />There's no doubt an audience for this record, so my two-star rating should be read in context. But people approaching it from the same background and with the same expectations as me might be disappointed.

0985081821|5.0|My 9 Reader 'Hot-Buttons' on THE FOREVER GIRL by Rebecca Hamilton    1. World's Immersion:  We arrive in Sophia's \\"ordinary world\\" of Belle Meadow, Colorado, an isolated mountain-town, inside Sophia's ostracized P.O.V. working at a diner surrounded by small town scrutiny. We follow Sophia into her private world, inside the generations old house she inherited from her Grandfather, or to Paloma's intriguing metaphysical shop with its special curiosities.  We learn of Sophia's world as she stumbles on the realization, that supernatural beings are converging all around her, and her supposedly ordinary, small town.  This gradually opening aperture into the world of \\"The Forever Girl\\" is how Rebecca Hamilton introduces her new series to enthrall Readers...    2. Characters/Icons:  In SOPHIA, we have a heroine who instantly identifies Readers with the outsider icon, a status many of us can empathize with.  Sophia's core secret grants both an emotional 'Achilles Heel' as well as seeds for expanding into her personal mythology. Sophia's adherence to Wiccan practices and ethical considerations offers Readers a female character with a refreshing, positive-minded Occult P.O.V.    CHARLES, Sophia's romantic challenge, is a refined supernatural being.  He captivates Sophia's intrigue with his knowing sensuality.  He is the first guy who gets what Sophia is thinking and feeling. The other mix in this cocktail, is that Charles' very nature poses risk, even threat toward Sophia's ultimate love or doom.  RH takes time in exploring the magnetism between Sophia and Charles as two very different supernatural beings.  Can Sophia and Charles safely co-exist, in a dangerous relationship they are both intent on deepening?    IVORY:  She is Sophia's friend, the reflection character who defies such designation, with her own complexity of emotional journey.  Ivory is not just there listening to Sophia as her BFF sounding board.  Ivory, as a supporting character, surprised me with the depth towhich her own mystery eventually unfolds.    PALOMA:  Sophia has a mentor-figure in Paloma, her guide with access to mystical items beyond the reach of just any ordinary shop-keeper, and indispensable to the heroine on a mythic journey.    MRS. FRANKLIN:  The counter-point to Sophia's gentle approach to Spirit...in the form of judgmental bile and Mrs. Franklin's own orchestrated perversion of religious orthodoxy.  This cult figure has her baleful eye set on righteously striking Sophia down for 'the good of the town.'    THALIA:  A vampiric vixen with a fang for Charles, and a harpy's derision for Sophia.  When Thalia discovers Charles and Sophia's secret, she is fully capable of driving a stake through the heart of their relationship.    VALERIA & HENRY LIETTE:  Charles' parents made him what he is; and what he is, becomes a key to the warning between he and Sophia.  The Liettes appear in Sophia's life like a visitation from some dark fairy-tale...passing by for creature comforts and a mysterious exchange.    3. Structural Appointments:  RH oscillates between Sophia's first person view-point, and breaking away into historical back-story branches, following supporting characters in third person narrative.  With this structure, RH simultaneously involves Readers in Sophia's personal quest, also freeing perspective to advance the story in broadening strokes.  Historical layers add substantial support to the core, modern-day story.    4. \\"Visuality\\":  RH is adept at capturing essential details.  When in more intimate scenes, dialogue and gesture rule, focusing awareness into noting emotional/psychological details shifting behind what is expressed.  RH is selective and tasteful with beats of sensory awareness or aesthetic richness.  RH capably displays her flair for writing with a sense like a movie lens, as dramatic action takes the stage.    5. Thematic/Mythic Appeals:  Is \\"Forever\\" desirable...unattainable...or a curse?  Facing an unbalanced relationship of secrets kept, becomes a possible deal-breaker in Sophia's emotional opening.  How does Sophia bridge the vast gap between 'here today, gone tomorrow' and 'forever?'    One key to my higher interest in Rebecca Hamilton's \\"Forever Girl,\\" is the theme of Personal Inner Power.  It is the way that RH directs this journey of a young woman transforming her curse into currency, that fills this story with promise.  The author accomplishes this Occult tale with good taste intact.  I have had my own positive experiences with Wicca, and understand The Craft to be empowering for women and men, nature-based, and benevolent.  Sophia is a heroine I could identify with on this level:  magic manifests from within.    6. Story Flow:  \\"The Forever Girl\\" unravels in a gradual unfurling of mysteries comprising its elements; but RH's telling of it, is economically achieved.  Readers are kept involved with pace, clarity, immediacy of details, and progression of a world's depth.  There are no bogged-down sections where one may lose connectivity.    I enjoyed my discovery of what lay in wait, because the details were freshly devised.  I naturally wanted to journey with the characters' discoveries, as the story deepened its scope while maintaining its mystery.  I trusted in Rebecca's vision, and went along for the fantastical ride.    7. Innovation/Genre-Blend:  Rebecca has added an extra dimension to her hybrid-genre, \\"An Urban Fantasy / Paranormal Romance,\\" with \\"of the Occult.\\"  There has always been an under-the-surface cultural fear of this supernatural being, the Witch, as it has been expressed in popular literature.  It is only in recent years, that progressions have been made by writers such as Rebecca Hamilton, looking for the depth in this Wise Woman Iconography.    How to connect the vampire, were-creature, ghost, witch and any other supernatural creatures of diverse differences, into a frame-work that is fantasy-grounded, and more significant, than arbitrary inclusion?  HOW the author accomplishes this creation, is also what I feel is fresh about \\"The Forever Girl.\\"    8. Author's Voice/Language:  Rebecca Hamilton expresses an indie alternative view-point, rare to be depicted in mainstream publishing.  RH's writing-style displays lyrical details with an elegant clarity.  RH shows her writing range, in that she confidently shifts between intimate character exchanges, to a narrator's  unraveling of historical back-story, to a surprising ferocity in the violence depicted when ever it arrives.  RH strikes harder in an action sequence than I actually expected, granting a gravity of threat to Charles and Sophia's challenges.  \\"The Forever Girl\\" is not a gory reality, but still an appropriately dangerous one to enjoy.    9.  The After Resonance:  Sophia is an inspired avatar for demographics of younger and older Wiccan/Goth/Alternative sub-cultures. These are Reading audiences that have always sought out edgy, underground Books to exemplify their cultural P.O.V.  Sophia taps into a very large Reading audience, which when considered separate, may at an earlier time have been considered 'Fringe.'  They are in fact, cultural prime movers, and a big part of making Comicon such a mainstream phenomenon.  Sophia's journey to personal empowerment through recognizable growing pains and opening sexuality, gives her universal New Adult appeal.    I want to point out that RH's book is in the \\"New Adult\\" range of audience, a flex-period in maturing growth experiences or life experimentation.  Key characters are seeking their adult lives, chiefly between late teens to the mid 20's, or just before entering their 30's.  In New Adult, RH was able to offer a more sophisticated Occult angle, a more adult sensuality between Sophia and Charles, graphic conflict; and yet, a YA audience would be entirely in range for appreciating the novel's youthful voice in Sophia.  Rebecca tweeted about the \\"New Adult Genre,\\" being the first to inform me that this range has a niche designationin publishing, as \\"Young Adult\\" popularly has.    I feel that in Sophia, Readers have a feminine character with heart and mystique, an accessible vulnerability to identify with, yet a willful heroism so as to engage a Reader on numerous levels.  Sophia is also not being constantly saved by Charles, her male counterpart; because Readers will find, in all key points where actions must be taken, that it is Sophia herself who rises to the challenges of her own growth arc.  RH has also fashioned a world of cohesive, supporting mythology that opens a well-latticed capacity for fulfilling further stories.  It is easy to foresee \\"The Forever Girl\\" series deepening its supernatural potential...and fan-base.    Andre Hirsch Todorovich, Transcendentalist Writer ~ [...]

B00005NMXI|4.0|Exaggerations aside, one thing must be said: this guy did know how to write a good song. Never mind the arguments about who is the best songwriter of the world. His music is not about making comparisons, it's about being comprehended. And when it comes to Townes Van Zandt, that doesn't take a lot of effort from the listener. You just need a few verses, some seconds of music and, if you really concentrate on what you hear as you listen (note that these are two very different things), that's it. He's got you.<br />His discography, however, may cause some people to be confused. Many songs on the first two albums (which constitute disc 1 of this box set) were recorded with different arrangements on the following ones. Maybe because he knew they didn't need all that radio-friendly production they were submitted to. They could be simple as hell and still mean a lot (or even more than they already did). That also makes the task of pointing out his best or most accomplished record a bit hard. But in the end, it all depends on your personal taste as a listener, because the consistence of Van Zandt's universal approach remains intact.<br />This box set includes his first 7 albums and part of the live recording that was released on the double album Live at The Old Quarter. Also, you get an extensive booklet containing lots of pictures, biographical notes and track-by-track comments. Note that there are two versions of this release: the first one, from 2002, has a book-like format, while the 2005 edition comes in an actual box. But I believe that both carry the same content (although I can't be sure if the box has the same visual appeal and graphic content the book does).<br />But enough with technicalities. The music here surpasses everything. Van Zandt had something that is inherent only to those with a high sense of life and perception. He seems to understand you as much as you will probably understand him, and that is what makes a songwriter so influential and respected. You relate to his thoughts the more you give them a listen, despite the irregular discography. Wherever you start this journey, the aftermath will probably be worth the trip.u relate to his thoughts the more you give them a listen, despite the irregular discography. There is color thrown in here and there, but this is primarily a black and white, film, and isn't that fitting. If there's one thing to say about the White Stripes, it's that they keep the formula simple. Simple colors, simple instruments, and simple song structures. But, much like black and white photography and video, it's all about what is done within the walls of the limitations. I wish I'd had this book before leaving college - it would have saved me making many career mistakes.<br /><br />This book encourages readers to be action-oriented and act like an entrepreneur, taking responsibility for personal development. B0000025GW|3.0|Slow Train Coming is Bob Dylan's religious album, or rather, the beginning of a religious-based period of albums that progressed into the early 80's.  If THIS is what I can expect for the rest of this particular period, well... I probably won't even bother.  This is disappointing enough.<br /><br />\\"Gotta Serve Somebody\\" has lyrics that explain no matter WHO you are, you're gonna have to serve *somebody*.  The slow-paced disco beats and the straight forward lyrics leave a lot to be desired, however.  The disco connection unfortunately sets the pace for the rest of the experience which, judging by the numerous positive reviews here on amazon, might make this the most overrated album in Dylan's discography.  \\"Precious Angel\\" is generic mid-tempo plodding nonsense from beginning to end.  This is by FAR the worst Dylan album up to this point.  Self Portrait had variety at least.  Slow Train Coming, musically, is a product of the times- the dreaded days of the disco movement.  Yuck.  The \\"Shine your light on me\\" chorus is almost sickeningly uncharacteristic of Dylan.  You can tell he's forcing himself to sing such a line, and I swear I can almost sense that he's about ready to break out laughing during the chorus.  The only compliment I can give is that the Jerry Garcia-inspired guitar playing is decent.<br /><br />\\"I Believe In You\\" includes a melody so soft that it's basically a lullaby with terribly bland, simplistic and lovey dovey lyrics.  Anyone who believes Dylan can't write vocal melodies would have a good point here.  Normally I'd disagree with those people but not in this case.  \\"Slow Train\\" has a somewhat groovy rhythm, okay lyrics about a woman down in Alabama and figuring out and establishing your principles.  The guitar solo is actually really good.  Best song so far.  \\"Gonna Change My Way of Thinking\\" is a MAJOR low point.  Unsatisfying and disappointing Joe Walsh style mid-tempo hard rock with a VERY lackluster heavy guitar riff chugging along for the entire duration.  I'm mystified how so many Dylan fans can easily forgive such a failed experiment.<br /><br />\\"Do Right To Me Baby (Do Unto Others)\\" is where the religious lyrics are beginning to overstay their welcome as they're now becoming excessively irritating.  The bigger problem however is that there's *still* very little in the way of exciting musical ideas taking place here.  The songwriting is definitely uninteresting to me.  Just because Dylan finally revealed his feelings on religious matters doesn't automatically mean these songs are a pleasure to hear.  \\"When You Gonna Wake Up\\" is lyrically quite tedious and straight forward despite Dylan telling it like it is.  I'm sorry but this song sounds WAY too similar to Dire Strait's \\"In the Gallery\\" released a year before.  Same vocal mannerisms and punchy rhythm.  I *do* like how the tempo picks up during the chorus at least.  Yes and I'm aware that members of Dire Straits participated on this album so Mark Knopfler's style was bound to be noticeable either way but eh... I also have mixed feelings when the Band joined Dylan.  When you combine two artists with a distinctive style the result is that it usually limits the potential of *both* artists, or rather, encourages them both to hold back their full scope of creativity which is the case here with Dylan and Knopfler.<br /><br />\\"Man Gave Name to All the Animals\\" is a reggae style tune with a strong appearance of female vocals.  Dylan's voice is exceptionally hoarse but both the verse AND the chorus are oddly melodic and appealing to me.  This song almost sounds cute!  Definitely the most unusual song on the album.  It avoids resembling generic disco beats at least.  \\"When He Returns\\" is a piano ballad but... this is no ordinary piano playing!  It's VERY spiritual, uplifting, and emotionally a highlight.  Maybe even the best song on the album both lyrically and musically.  Great way to end the album.<br /><br />Man, I don't know guys.  Songs such as \\"Gotta Serve Somebody\\", \\"Precious Angel\\", \\"I Believe In You\\" and ESPECIALLY \\"Gonna Change My Way of Thinking\\" are REALLY unappealing to me.  I suppose the rest of the material is passable but come on, Dylan's first religious-themed album is hardly flawless.  Look beyond the lyrics and notice the mediocre songwriting covering up most of the album.  On a final note just let me mention that I have no problem whatsoever with the lyrics.  Dylan covers religion exceptionally well and his conversion to Christianity is commendable, but I wouldn't expect any less from the master of creative wordplay.  Besides, every Christian I've ever known has always been super polite and supportive towards me and my decisions.  The problem I have with the album is that the music doesn't match the lyrics.  Not many (if any) artists entered the dreaded, horribly dated days of the disco movement and came out with flying colors.  It's just impossible for me to look beyond the generic music and focus on the lyrics.

0307352145|5.0|Throughout my life I have had well meaning people telling me I should want to socialise all the time and that there is something wrong with me because I would almost always prefer to be curled up at home with a book. I have always had to battle for my quiet time against my nearest and dearest who think I should want to be with them. If I spend too much time with other people I start to lose sight of who I am and I have to spend a few hours by myself to recharge my batteries.<br /><br />This is something I've always known about myself. When I first heard the word `introvert' and understood its meaning I knew it applied to me. But being an introvert was something that you just didn't talk about because being the life and soul of the party was the ideal. To get on at work and in life you need to be outgoing and willing to spend all your time talking to other people. The Western world values extroversion and introverts don't count partly because it is difficult for them to make themselves heard.<br /><br />The book discusses research in the field and how the quality of introversion is displayed in the world. I found it fascinating to read about those who predicted the last recession and who said that what the banks were doing was extremely risky. Banks were staffed by extroverts who liked taking risks and they didn't want to listen to the quiet people sitting in the corner poring over graphs, charts and figures and predicting doom and gloom. What this book shows is that we need both introverts and extroverts to get a balance between excessive risk and excessive caution.<br /><br />I was intrigued to learn that it is not only human beings who are introverts or extroverts, animals and even fish have those qualities too. Guppies which live in areas of rivers where Pike feed have different personalities from those who live in the same rivers in places where Pike don't swim. If one type of Guppy moves from one area to another it takes about twenty years for their personalities to change as personality traits in Guppies seem to be inherited. In some circumstances introversion is a trait which is necessary for survival as it imbues caution and careful assessment of risk - in this case Pike swimming by and looking for a tasty meal.<br /><br />Various studies have been carried out which show that introverts engaged on creative work do a lot better if they are allowed to work on their own in peace and quiet rather than being forced to work in an open plan office. Yet the trend is for everyone to work in open plan offices. Some companies are gradually realising they need to provide both environments for their staff so that people who need peace and quiet to do their best work can find space to shut themselves away from the crowd.<br /><br />This book is well written and an interesting and lively read for anyone who is an introvert or who lives or works with introverts. I found it a fascinating and thought provoking read - most especially the chapter on bringing up naturally introverted children. I found myself wanting to stand up and scream `YES YES YES' at the top of my voice as I recognised myself in so many of the examples quoted. B005S9EJ14|3.0|This film is not the first to employ the plot device of a bunch of ill-matched strangers thrown together with their individual stories, who develop self-knowledge through interaction with each other in a new environment. The setting here is contemporary Jaipur and the characters are a bunch of fish-out-of-water Brits in their twilight years; a new twist on the old storyline. So what's `The Best Exotic Marigold Hotel' like as a viewing experience?<br /><br />The answer is generally good, with minor reservations. The Brit A-list cast of autumn-years players is made, either by skilled make-up or even more skilled close-up camerawork, to look - well, so old, especially when contrasted to the youthful dynamism of some of the Indian cast. Is this supposed to be a not-so-subtle metaphor about these two nations and their current global profiles?<br /><br />The Rajasthan locations are brought to life by superb camerawork and editing and a great eye for detail. The film captures Jaipur to perfection, though of course you don't get the overpowering olfactory cocktail of cattle, incense and spices, perfumes, diesel fumes and industrial pollution of modern India on film, nor the searing >40C dry heat in the summer.<br /><br />At times TBEMH has the feel of a Mike Lee film, with his technique of allowing the actors to determine story development by their own real-life interactions in front of the camera. At other times it's obviously scripted; funny, poignant and touching and occasionally a bit heavy-handed. The characters rather predictably develop, learn and change as they interact with India and with each other: some adapt to their new home like ducks to water and with a sense of adventure; others don't fare so well, and some take time to eventually feel at home. A sub-plot between the young, enthusiastic and eternally optimistic hotel manager (Dev Patel) and his pretty moped-riding, call-centre-employed girlfriend (Tena Desai) is used to explore the struggle between the new India with self-assertive young professionals living by western values and the traditional controlling influence of their Indian families. Unfortunately the confrontational resolution of this conflict will be seen by some viewers as melodramatic and trite - actually it's positively toe-curling, and that's all I'm going to say.<br /><br />Overall, not a bad film but short of excellent. See it for the veteran cast turning in some fine performances, a good-but-not-great script containing a few surprises amongst the clich&eacute;s (cricket playing street kids, Indian university graduates working in call-centres, the iniquities of the Hindu caste system) and some great one-liners: an overall feel-good and life-affirming viewing experience set in stunning Jaipur locations.ndia with self-assertive young professionals living by western values and the traditional controlling influence of their Indian families. 0140288503|5.0|It's my firm opinion that if you made every single American read both Black Hawk Down and Generation Kill, you would have a populace that's far more understanding of our military, and one that was much closer to the reality than the hyperbolic extremes of \\"ultra-patriotic America defenders\\" or \\"baby-killing murderers\\". While Generation Kill focuses on the soldiers themselves, Black Hawk Down focuses (as its subtitle suggests) on war itself, on both a macro and micro level. Bowden's subject is the Battle of Mogadishu, in which a daytime raid that was supposed to be an in-and-out subject capture mission became a massive siege in which multiple American helicopters were shot down and the soldiers were forced to defend themselves against a city literally full of attackers. The battle itself was chaos (to put it mildly), and Bowden manages the difficult task of conveying that chaos while still keeping numerous threads of action clear in the reader's head. More than that, Bowden ably switches between the battle as viewed through the eyes of those who fought it and the battle as seen on a wider scope, and through that switching, he conveys exactly how little things can spiral out of control in a battle scenario. Black Hawk Down is a harrowing read, and rightfully so; here is a situation where a relatively small fighting force struggles against an entire city that rallies against them, and Bowden perfectly immerses the reader in that terrifying situation. At the same time, he often switches to the Somali perspective, a choice that both widens the book's perspective as well as helping the reader to understand many of the reasons the battle escalated as much as it did. Black Hawk Down earns its secondary title well; here is a book about how technology has changed the face of war, and how American domination often can be its own worst enemy. It's also a book about those who fight our wars and what kind of people they are, as well as the ways in which even the tiniest mistake in a battle can change everything. It's a harrowing, riveting book, one that both condemns and praises the military, taking an even-handed look at the battle and trying to understand how things got to where they did. it avoids easy answers, refuses to pass judgment, and merely documents; yet, for all that, there's no way to walk away from the book and not have your opinion shaped and your beliefs changed. A superb book that should be required for any American, period.n a battle can change everything. B0071XO8RA|4.0|I am partial to fantasy as a genre and only rarely dip into science fiction, but this was well worth my time. I was surprised that this was a self-published book, but some of the flaws I found in the writing make sense now.<br />Overall, Howey builds an amazing, dystopic world that you can completely lose yourself in. The world he builds is rich with details both in imagery and scientific. As a tech/science junkie, I bought into the science part of this fiction totally. It is well-planned and thoroughly thought out. I'm not sure if this is the case if I was looking at one book in particular, but reading 1 through 5 as a unit, I can state the previous comment with absolute certainty. There isn't any detail about the world or the people that leaves you hanging.<br />In terms of plot, I was thoroughly pleased with not only a satisfyingly exciting plot, but also with mundane parts that were always fraught with an underlying tension. Subplot threads are wrapped up nicely by the end of the 5th book and allow for you to focus entirely on the main plot that will continue in the 6th book.<br />Here is my major complaint: Howey needs work on character building. All of the books are written in third-person limited with occasional jumps into the minds of the characters. Unfortunately, sometimes in an effort to reinforce certain character traits, the internal dialogues can feel ham-fisted. I definitely think that this is his greatest weakness: he can get inside of his characters' heads, but is noticeably uncomfortable there. This is mostly true when his characters' lives aren't endangered or there isn't some great swell of emotion to work through. It can make the internal dialogue about the mundane aspects of the characters' lives a bit of a slog to read through. It is possible that the reason stems from almost no divorce of the narrator's voice and the character's voice, but our internal voices are never as clear as the narrator, so sometimes the language feels stiff.<br />Ultimately a wonderful series that I hope Howey continues to write. Maybe he'll get picked up by Tor....one can only hope!

B006OAB3ME|1.0|There have always been two competing, conflicting trends pervading Paul McCartney's musical career. One, he undeniably is a great musical talent. He can rock when he wants to and at one time was a superb tune smith. Another trend, a contradictory one, is that he is a rank sentimentalist.<br /><br />He was careful to balance the two trends in the beginning. This trend surfaced early in Paul's career with the song, \\"Yesterday.\\" Lennon was incensed by this song, and considered Paul a traitor to rock and roll with the release of this song. But Paul rocked on with other Beatles songs like \\"I'm Down\\" and \\"She's a Woman.\\" That last one was actually by all accounts a true collaborative effort. No matter, the point is that if he put out a sentimental song, he would balance it afterwards with a rocker.<br /><br />This is not to say that sentiment is wrong. Far from it. When PM was at the zenith of his solo musical talents, shortly after he left the Beatles, the results were magnificent. The mistake that many music lovers made when listening to \\"Silly Love Songs,\\" is that they were confusing Paul the rocker and Paul the rank sentimentalist, and wanting him to rock instead of waxing sentiment.<br /><br />There were two other trends which at times worked against each other. Paul has always been willing to experiment. This is a good thing, and can yield good reasons. McCartney II, for example, was Paul's experiment into electronics which was one of John Lennon's personal favorite albums Paul released. It showed a lot of courage, it rocks and was a good, if underrated, album. He has since released efforts with the Firemen, which is respectable ambient music.<br /><br /> But there is another trend which is problematic. He has an almost maniacal impulse to churn out product, no matter how good or bad it may be. This rarely produces good results; in fact, more likely than not, it produces garbage. Much of his efforts at classical music fits into this category. The music is forgettable -- it really isn't his music at all. Paul McCartney, like many rock musicians, cannot read or write musical notation. Consequently, when PM releases a classical release, it is usually a collaboration with a recognized musician, who has written down PM's musical ideas, much like dictation. This irrational desire to release albums and songs is a hit and miss thing. It is usually a \\"miss,\\" however, and produces garbage. Like this album.<br /><br />Words really cannot convey how bad this album truly is. The crooning I don't mind, but McCartney doesn't croon very well. He falters, he hesitates, his voice cracks -- not an inflected, intentional, crack, but a crack of a seventy-year old washed-up rocker. This album is an embarrassment; it is an outrage; it is just a poor rendition of lounge singing. Paul is a complex, complicated musical talent, much more than John ever was, but this album is surprisingly simple. It is simply BAD.<br /><br />While listening to this album my thoughts were with the old Saturday Night Live. In the early shows the troupe would do skits called \\"Bad Theatre,\\" Bad Poetry,\\" and the like, portraying, well, a play or poetry poorly done, usually hosted by Dan Akroyd. While listening to this album I thought, \\"If the old SNL were around they would call this  \\"Bad Music.'\\"<br /><br />I also thought of that Bill Murray character -- I couldn't concentrate on the music, it was so terrible -- of the cheesy lounge singer in the glitter tux and slicked back hair, who, of course, thought he was good and cool, but was, of course, terrible.<br /><br />Then I thought, well, maybe Paul is releasing this album for the sake of irony, as a kitsch experiement, a parody on lounge singing and lounge singers. No, he really thinks this stuff is good and he's doing a good job. Well, it is not and he doesn't.<br /><br /> The only parody is the title of the album, Kisses on the Bottom. What's up with the title of the album anyway? This is apparently Paul's attitude for anyone foolish enough to buy this album. If you don't like my album, well, you can just,  ....<br /><br /> This is not to say that all interpretation of standards are all bad. Far from it. Rod Stewart is a good example of an ex-rocker who is successful in his interpretation of song standards. But Rod Stewart makes no pretensions of being a rock and roll star anymore. He left that persona, sadly, years ago. But he is good at what he does. Stewart's voice is perfectly matched to sing these classics; he is a befitting successor to Tony Bennett.<br /><br /> But Paul McCartney singing lounge songs?  Paleeez. Other reviews have noted that his voice is creepy. There is some truth to this. His voice lacks the intimacy that is must be present whenever engaging in this type of singing. So Paul is deliberately softening his voice to give it that soft intimacy, to make it more personal. What he has done, of course, is exposed the natural weaknesses of advancing old age, making his voice sound foreign and, yes, a little creepy. All those years of yelping like Little Richard have taken a toll on his voice. To me, though, his singing is just plain bad. Bad, bad, bad.<br /><br /> And this is not to say that the album is totally irredeemable. As in all CDs, if you inadvertently buy this album, it will make an excellent coaster, and can be used as a reflector, or a car ornament. Or you might do the Green thing and simply recycle this CD to your local recycle center. This is probably the best use of this CD. It is not fit for listening, so do something contructive and good for the environment. It's no good as music. It is a joke, sadly on anyone who buys this CD.one foolish enough to buy this album. If you don't like my album, well, you can just,  ....<br /><br /> This is not to say that all interpretation of standards are all bad. Far from it. Rod Stewart is a good example of an ex-rocker who is successful in his interpretation of song standards. But Rod Stewart makes no pretensions of being a rock and roll star anymore. He left that persona, sadly, years ago. But he is good at what he does. Stewart's voice is perfectly matched to sing these classics; he is a befitting successor to Tony Bennett.<br /><br /> But Paul McCartney singing lounge songs?  Paleeez. Other reviews have noted that his voice is creepy. There is some truth to this. His voice lacks the intimacy that is must be present whenever engaging in this type of singing. So Paul is deliberately softening his voice to give it that soft intimacy, to make it more personal. What he has done, of course, is exposed the natural weaknesses of advancing old age, making his voice sound foreign and, yes, a little creepy. 0060955775|5.0|One cannot judge a book by its cover, the American idiom suggests; as I sat reading a passerby interjects:  \\"Geez, that title sounds so morbid\\". On the contrary the book dwells not on death but on the joys inherent in life. The book is not about suicide, as it seems- it's about living, and learning to live.<br /><br />On November 11, 1997, Veronika decided that the moment to kill herself had arrived.  Feeling unfulfilled & vitriol consumed (poisoned by bitterness), she decides that she would be better off dead.  By our society's standards she has everything she needs to be happy. She is living but she is not alive. She is a 24 year old young woman that hasn't yet discovered the purpose of life and she concludes that a life governed by the expectations of others is a farce, which has reduced her to a shallow empty shell, essentially a breathing marionette.<br /><br />And I quote: \\"The great problem with poisoning by Bitterness was that the passions -- hatred, love, despair, enthusiasm, curiosity -- also ceased to manifest themselves. After a while, the embittered person felt no desire at all. They lacked the will either to live or to die, that was the problem.\\"<br /><br />As told to Veronika- and I quote: \\"You are someone who is different, but who wants to be the same as everyone else. And that in my view is a serious illness. God chose you to be different. Why are you disappointing God with this kind of attitude?\\"<br /><br />Veronika, was not content with the regular monotony of life that most of us live and needed a greater purpose to be alive, so after a failed suicidal attempt is shocked to awaken in Villete, a Slovenian mental hospital, only to be told that while she was not successful in dying she has committed herself to death as the overdose damaged her heart, as she now has but a few days left to live. Insightfully; it takes the imminence of death to make her discover that being alive is more than living.<br /><br />Thusly; life becomes precious when you realize that it will end. The premise of the book on one level is that we can only appreciate life when it is fully lived and/or is about to end, and additionally, sends a powerful message to all who live life attempting to conform as opposed to living.<br /><br />These allotted days turn out to be the only time in her life that she truly lives wholeheartedly. Energetically, emotionally, artistically, passionately & sexually, as she plunges into a series of explorations and adventures that allow her to become a radiating & loving independent being and not the product of societal expectations. While not advocating mayhem or disorder, the author uses Veronica's journey to exemplify that total compliance with external demands doesn't only regulate existence- it also strips it of value.  And I quote: \\"You have two choices: to control your mind or to let your mind control you.\\"<br /><br />This is a magical tale about striving to always discover new emotions, feelings, & reasons for living.  It is a beautiful yet tragic miracle (of fiction) that I would recommend to anyone who enjoys the trip of self discovery as this adventure speaks to all of us. I certainly left this book with more than I went into it with.  It resonated so powerfully with me, as it really moved me and inspired me to think - about intrinsic purpose, self truth, love, life and so much more. The author does encourage readers, through Veronika's example, to live each minute as if it might be your last, and treat your life like the precious gift it is. And I quote: \\"She would consider each day a miracle - which indeed it is, when you consider the number of unexpected things that could happen in each second of our fragile existences.\\"<br /><br />I do not, at this point want in any manner to spoil the read, but yes; the ending does have a remarkable 12th hour twist :)

B0044AS6BC|5.0|In the early stages of Stephen King's career, he wanted to put his own twist on some of the hallmarks of the horror genre, including vampires with Salem's Lot and the haunted house story, or in this case haunted hotel, with The Shining. This horrific tale of ghosts, possession, and murder ranks as one of King's all-time scariest, and spawned the Stanley Kubrick film that most regard as a classic in its own right. Most fans are aware that King was disappointed by some aspects of that film, including its failure to establish that Jack Torrance arrives at the Overlook Hotel as a good father, a dedicated family man who would never think of bringing harm to his wife or son. In many ways, Torrance reflects the pressures and frustration King himself experienced as a young husband and father, a character prone to the same occasional impatience or anger as any normal person. However, when those frailties are placed in the hands of the malevolent forces at work in the Overlook, they become warped and distorted. Jack's descent into insanity is a gradual process initiated and controlled by external influences, a point left ambiguous in Kubrick's film. The film even hints at an inherent madness in the character before ever reaching the Overlook, so the differences from King's story are significant. Kubrick also excludes King's central metaphor of the boiler, which Jack has to release the pressure from periodically to avoid a powerful explosion. It's used in the novel to represent Jack's escalating rage as his mental instability progresses and is key to the book's plot, particularly the climax. I'm not knocking the filmmaking of Kubrick, but like most King fans, I prefer the novel. If you're looking for a scare and haven't already read The Shining, imagine the possibilities of an enormous hotel filled with malicious ghosts, a small, young family snowed in and isolated from all civilization, and the writing of a young Stephen King. One of King's top five novels.

B000002G7E|5.0|Whenever I listen to The Velvet Underground I'm not entirely certain that I get it.  By this, I mean that if I had never heard of this band would I even give it the time of day?  I frequently think this whenever I read books or watch movies that are well reviewed as well.  Do I really like what I am listening to or seeing, or do I just like what the piece of art has been portrayed as through critics and the media.<br />I try my best to give all types of music a fair shake because it's important to be open minded about these sorts of things.  Whether it is rap, jazz, hardcore metal music, etc. I believe every kind of music has something to offer, and it's important to at least try listening.<br />What does this have to do with the infamous White Light/White Heat?  Well, at first listen this is probably one of the ugliest pieces of noise to ever attack your ears.  To steal a quote from Wikipedia (I know not the best source), John Cale said of this album, \\"a very rabid record...The first one had some gentility, some beauty. The second one was consciously anti-beauty.\\"<br />My girlfriend, whom I subject to all the music I test out, said that the music on this record is brutal.  Not in a good way either.  And maybe it is brutal, not only in it's sonic power (there is plenty of energy to spare if nothing else), but also in how difficult it can be to handle.  The guitars scream, Loud Reed howls vulgarities and nonsensical lyrics, and the band in general seems intent on playing jam band music from hell.  The album isn't so much a listening experience as it is a violent, noisy ordeal that may cause you a massive migraine.<br />So why would I give it five stars?  Because I keep coming back to it.  I get tired of typical pop music and the instant gratification that most songs give listeners in this day and age.  The first time I listened to it I didn't understand it.  The second time I was intrigued (I know how pretentious that sounds but it's true).  The third time I loved it.  Now, this brand of music permeates my thoughts whenever I check out new bands.  Simply put, it has me in its grip.<br />This doesn't even mention the fact that it's a rock record that laid the foundation for punk rock and everything that followed afterwards.  Is that to say punk rock never would have come about without this album? Certainly not.  But this band had the foresight to plant the seeds for that type of music and many musicians such as Henry Rollins cite it as a major influence.<br />So maybe I wouldn't have given this album a second listen had I not heard about the band beforehand.  However, I wouldn't have heard about the band if they didn't pave the way for much of the rock music that followed after it.<br />So, I suggest that if you get the opportunity, sit down, maybe pour yourself a glass of wine (or something rougher), and play this album from start to finish (preferably on vinyl).  But be forewarned, it is a harrowing experience upon first listen.  Then after you have finished, let your opinion sit for a couple of days and don't touch it again.  Then listen to it again.  Then, listen to it once more after that.  If you don't like it after that you may not ever like it.  But this is an album that, like a fire, needs a chance to breathe.  And if you let it do so, you might find yourself getting hooked into its mania.  It has certainly made an impression on me.

One might be worried that the Tudors would get stale with 6 different wives to choose from; fear not - the show has so many different aspects that you will not get bored. From politics at Court to war, from love to death, from King Henry's outrageous temper to the political machinations of those around him, the show does not fail to surprise.<br /><br />The 6 wives are often known through the refrain of \\"divorced, beheaded, died; divorced, beheaded, survived\\" and the show details each and every aspect of that fun. Queen Catherine of Aragon is spanish and refuses to give up on her role, ultimately dying without any closure. Queen Anne is a wanton lascivious woman who schemes to get her desires. We dont learn too much of Jane Seymour, except she has the king's only son. Anne of Cleves is described as horseface yet is not unattractive, but the King divorces her anyway and keeps her on as a close friend. Catherine Howard is young and sleeps around with his chamberman, and ends up getting beheaded for her sins. Finally, Catherine Parr, who you may recognize as Joely Richardson from Nip/Tuck on Fx, ends up with the most longevity and survives despite some scares due to her religious beliefs.<br /><br />Religion is discussed at length here, and the King's views change over time, but his penchance for blood and gore rule out. Many people are sent to the tower, burned, beheaded, dragged through town, or simply executed for whatever reason the King chooses. Some are as simple as not acting \\"lordly\\" while others are schemers or trying to subvert the King's chosen religion. Political intrigue also plays a very large part, with many players trying to get their own goals achieved or pushing their ambitions to the King.<br /><br />The scenery is lush, with many outdoor scenes shot showing the King's grounds, foreign countries, and English countrysides. The clothing appears to be indicative of the times and much thought has gone into this. The people are well-suited for their roles and I rarely had any complaints that an actor under or over-acted their lines.<br /><br />It's a fun show. I truly enjoyed it and wished there was more!

True to HBO standards, most episodes include at least one instance of mind-shattering violence (see a particularly explosive headshot in the first episode) and graphic sexuality. Beyond these adult-TV 'staples,' the show is filled to the brim with freakishly good entertainment. The environments are marvelously detailed. The boardwalk sizzles with seedy glitz and depth. He possesses little of the grit or dirty fervor we might expect from a name like Nucky. Still, he is off-set by the electric Michael Pitt and the very talented Kelly McDonald whose temperance movement widow performance is one of the best female leads in recent memory (and the show's only female lead of note, providing the only empathetic center for progressive audience members...and women in general). He performs an interrogation at one point that makes 24's Jack Bauer tame by comparison. For anyone interesting in prohibition era gangster culture and not turned off by violence, profanity and a good bit of Non-PC dialogue/situations (including a few nasty 'dwarf' boxing matches and lots of derogatory language toward ladies), this is one show you ought not miss. It hits every historical note right on the head with bloody glory.
